     <p>
      The
      <b>
       history of Palestine
      </b>
      is the study of the past in the region of
      <a href="/wiki/Palestine" title="Palestine">
       Palestine
      </a>
      , generally defined as a geographic region in Western Asia between the Mediterranean Sea and the Jordan River (where
      <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">
       Israel
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/West_Bank" title="West Bank">
       West Bank
      </a>
      /
      <a href="/wiki/Palestine" title="Palestine">
       Palestine
      </a>
      is today), and various adjoining lands. Situated at a strategic point between
      <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">
       Europe
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">
       Asia
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">
       Africa
      </a>
      , and the birthplace of
      <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">
       Judaism
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">
       Christianity
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      the region has a long and tumultuous history as a crossroads for religion, culture, commerce, and politics. The Palestine region or parts of it have been controlled by numerous different peoples, including the
      <a href="/wiki/Canaan" title="Canaan">
       Paleo-Canaanites
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Amorites" title="Amorites">
       Amorites
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egyptian_New_Kingdom" title="Egyptian New Kingdom">
       Ancient Egyptians
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Israelites" title="Israelites">
       Israelites
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Moab" title="Moab">
       Moabites
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Ammon" title="Ammon">
       Ammonites
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Tjeker" title="Tjeker">
       Tjeker
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Philistines" title="Philistines">
       Philistines
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrian_Empire" title="Assyrian Empire">
       Assyrians
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Babylonian_Empire" title="Babylonian Empire">
       Babylonians
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">
       Persians
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Judea" title="Judea">
       Ancient Greeks
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Syria_Palaestina" title="Syria Palaestina">
       Romans
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">
       Byzantines
      </a>
      , (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Umayad" title="Umayad">
       Umayads
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abbasids" title="Abbasids">
       Abbasids
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seljuqs" title="Seljuqs">
       Seljuqs
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatimids" title="Fatimids">
       Fatimids
      </a>
      ),
      <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Jerusalem" title="Kingdom of Jerusalem">
       French Crusaders
      </a>
      , (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ayyubids" title="Ayyubids">
       Ayyubids
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mameluks" title="Mameluks">
       Mameluks
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">
       Ottoman Turks
      </a>
      ), the
      <a href="/wiki/Mandatory_Palestine" title="Mandatory Palestine">
       British
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hashemite_Kingdom_of_Jordan" title="Hashemite Kingdom of Jordan">
       Jordianians
      </a>
      (19481967, on the "
      <a href="/wiki/West_Bank" title="West Bank">
       West Bank
      </a>
      ") and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egyptian_Republic" title="Egyptian Republic">
       Egyptians
      </a>
      (in
      <a href="/wiki/Gaza_City" title="Gaza City">
       Gaza
      </a>
      ), and modern
      <a href="/wiki/Israelis" title="Israelis">
       Israelis
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Palestinians" title="Palestinians">
       Palestinians
      </a>
      . Other terms for the same area include
      <a href="/wiki/Canaan" title="Canaan">
       Canaan
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Zion" title="Zion">
       Zion
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Land_of_Israel" title="Land of Israel">
       Land of Israel
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Southern_Syria" title="Southern Syria">
       Southern Syria
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Jund_Filastin" title="Jund Filastin">
       Jund Filastin
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Outremer" title="Outremer">
       Outremer
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Holy_Land" title="Holy Land">
       Holy Land
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Southern_Levant" title="Southern Levant">
       Southern Levant
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The region was among the earliest in the world to see human habitation, agricultural communities and
      <a href="/wiki/Civilization" title="Civilization">
       civilization
      </a>
      . During the
      <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">
       Bronze Age
      </a>
      , independent
      <a href="/wiki/Canaan" title="Canaan">
       Canaanite
      </a>
      city-states were established, and were influenced by the surrounding civilizations of ancient Egypt,
      <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">
       Mesopotamia
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">
       Phoenicia
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Minoan_civilization" title="Minoan civilization">
       Minoan
      </a>
      Crete, and Syria. During 15501400
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BCE" title="BCE">
       BCE
      </a>
      , the Canaanite cities became vassals to the Egyptian
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Kingdom" title="New Kingdom">
       New Kingdom
      </a>
      who held power until the 1178 BCE
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Djahy" title="Battle of Djahy">
       Battle of Djahy (Canaan)
      </a>
      during the wider
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bronze_Age_collapse" title="Bronze Age collapse">
       Bronze Age collapse
      </a>
      . Modern archaeologists dispute parts of the Biblical tradition, the latest thinking being that the Israelites emerged from a dramatic social transformation that took place in the people of the central hill country of Canaan around 1200 BCE, with no signs of violent invasion or even of peaceful infiltration of a clearly defined ethnic group from elsewhere.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Finkelstein_3-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Finkelstein-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Philistines" title="Philistines">
       Philistines
      </a>
      , part of
      <a href="/wiki/Sea_Peoples" title="Sea Peoples">
       Sea Peoples
      </a>
      of Southern Europe, arrived and mingled with the local population, and according to Biblical tradition, the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_Monarchy" title="United Monarchy">
       United Kingdom of Israel
      </a>
      was established in 1020 BCE and split within a century to form the northern
      <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Israel_(Samaria)" title="Kingdom of Israel (Samaria)">
       Kingdom of Israel
      </a>
      , and the southern
      <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Judah" title="Kingdom of Judah">
       Kingdom of Judah
      </a>
      . The region became part of the
      <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">
       Neo-Assyrian Empire
      </a>
      from c. 740 BCE, which was itself replaced by the
      <a href="/wiki/Neo-Babylonian_Empire" title="Neo-Babylonian Empire">
       Neo-Babylonian Empire
      </a>
      in c. 627 BCE. A war with Egypt culminated in 586 BCE when Jerusalem was destroyed by the Babylonian king
      <a href="/wiki/Nebuchadnezzar_II" title="Nebuchadnezzar II">
       Nebuchadnezzar II
      </a>
      and the local leaders were
      <a href="/wiki/Babylonian_captivity" title="Babylonian captivity">
       deported to Babylonia
      </a>
      , only to be allowed to return under the
      <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">
       Achaemenid Empire
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      In the 330s BCE,
      <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">
       Alexander the Great
      </a>
      conquered the area now called Palestine, and the region changed hands numerous times during the
      <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Diadochi" title="Wars of the Diadochi">
       wars of the Diadochi
      </a>
      , ultimately joining the
      <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">
       Seleucid Empire
      </a>
      between 219 and 200 BCE. In 116 BCE, a Seleucid civil war resulted in the independence of certain regions including the minor
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hasmonean" title="Hasmonean">
       Hasmonean
      </a>
      principality in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Judean_Mountains" title="Judean Mountains">
       Judean Mountains
      </a>
      . From 110 BCE, the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hasmoneans" title="Hasmoneans">
       Hasmoneans
      </a>
      extended their authority over much of the area, creating a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Judean" title="Judean">
       Judean
      </a>
      
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samaritan" title="Samaritan">
       Samaritan
      </a>
      
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Idumaean" title="Idumaean">
       Idumaean
      </a>
      
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ituraean" title="Ituraean">
       Ituraean
      </a>
      
      <a href="/wiki/Galilean" title="Galilean">
       Galilean
      </a>
      alliance.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mortonsmith210_4-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-mortonsmith210-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Judean (Jewish, see
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ioudaioi" title="Ioudaioi">
       Ioudaioi
      </a>
      ) control over the wider region resulted in it also becoming known as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Judaea" title="Judaea">
       Judaea
      </a>
      , a term that had previously only referred to the smaller region of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Judean_Mountains" title="Judean Mountains">
       Judean Mountains
      </a>
      . During 7363 BCE, the
      <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">
       Roman Republic
      </a>
      extended its influence into the region in the
      <a href="/wiki/Third_Mithridatic_War" title="Third Mithridatic War">
       Third Mithridatic War
      </a>
      , conquering Judea in 63 BCE, and splitting the former Hasmonean Kingdom into five districts. In 70 CE,
      <a href="/wiki/Titus" title="Titus">
       Titus
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siege_of_Jerusalem_(70)" title="Siege of Jerusalem (70)">
       sacked Jerusalem
      </a>
      , resulting in the dispersal of the city's Jews and Christians to
      <a href="/wiki/Yavne" title="Yavne">
       Yavne
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Pella" title="Pella">
       Pella
      </a>
      . In 132 CE,
      <a href="/wiki/Hadrian" title="Hadrian">
       Hadrian
      </a>
      joined the province of Judaea with
      <a href="/wiki/Galilee" title="Galilee">
       Galilee
      </a>
      to form a new province and renamed it
      <a href="/wiki/Syria_Palaestina" title="Syria Palaestina">
       Syria Palaestina
      </a>
      , and Jerusalem was renamed "
      <a href="/wiki/Aelia_Capitolina" title="Aelia Capitolina">
       Aelia Capitolina
      </a>
      ". During 259272, the region fell under the rule of
      <a href="/wiki/Odaenathus" title="Odaenathus">
       Odaenathus
      </a>
      as King of the
      <a href="/wiki/Palmyrene_Empire" title="Palmyrene Empire">
       Palmyrene Empire
      </a>
      . Following the victory of Christian emperor
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constantine_I" title="Constantine I">
       Constantine
      </a>
      in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Civil_Wars_of_the_Tetrarchy_(306%E2%80%93324)" title="Civil Wars of the Tetrarchy (306324)">
       Civil Wars of the Tetrarchy (306324)
      </a>
      , the Christianization of the Roman Empire began, and in 326,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constantine_I" title="Constantine I">
       Constantine
      </a>
      's mother
      <a href="/wiki/Saint_Helena" title="Saint Helena">
       Saint Helena
      </a>
      visited
      <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">
       Jerusalem
      </a>
      and began the construction of churches and shrines. Palestine became a center of
      <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">
       Christianity
      </a>
      , attracting numerous monks and religious scholars. The
      <a href="/wiki/Samaritan_Revolts" title="Samaritan Revolts">
       Samaritan Revolts
      </a>
      during this period caused their near extinction.
     </p>
     <p>
      Palestine was conquered by the Islamic Empire following the 636 CE
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Yarmouk" title="Battle of Yarmouk">
       Battle of Yarmouk
      </a>
      during the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Syria" title="Muslim conquest of Syria">
       Muslim conquest of Syria
      </a>
      . In 661 CE, with the assassination of
      <a href="/wiki/Ali" title="Ali">
       Ali
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Muawiyah_I" title="Muawiyah I">
       Muawiyah I
      </a>
      became the uncontested Caliph of the Islamic World after being crowned in
      <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">
       Jerusalem
      </a>
      . In 691, the
      <a href="/wiki/Dome_of_the_Rock" title="Dome of the Rock">
       Dome of the Rock
      </a>
      became the world's first great work of Islamic architecture. The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Umayyad" title="Umayyad">
       Umayyad
      </a>
      were replaced by the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abbasids" title="Abbasids">
       Abbasids
      </a>
      in 750. From 878 Palestine was ruled from Egypt by semi-autonomous rulers for almost a century, beginning with
      <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_ibn_Tulun" title="Ahmad ibn Tulun">
       Ahmad ibn Tulun
      </a>
      , and ending with the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ikhshidid" title="Ikhshidid">
       Ikhshidid
      </a>
      rulers who were both buried in Jerusalem. The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatimid" title="Fatimid">
       Fatimids
      </a>
      conquered the region in 969. In 1073 Palestine was captured by the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Seljuq_Empire" title="Great Seljuq Empire">
       Great Seljuq Empire
      </a>
      , only to be recaptured by the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatimid" title="Fatimid">
       Fatimids
      </a>
      in 1098, who then lost the region to the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crusaders" title="Crusaders">
       Crusaders
      </a>
      in 1099. Their control of Jerusalem and most of Palestine lasted almost a century until defeat by
      <a href="/wiki/Saladin" title="Saladin">
       Saladin
      </a>
      's forces in 1187, after which most of Palestine was controlled by the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ayyubids" title="Ayyubids">
       Ayyubids
      </a>
      . A rump Crusader state in the northern coastal cities survived for another century, but, despite seven further Crusades, the Crusaders were no longer a significant power in the region. The
      <a href="/wiki/Mamluk_Sultanate_(Cairo)" title="Mamluk Sultanate (Cairo)">
       Mamluk Sultanate
      </a>
      was indirectly created in
      <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">
       Egypt
      </a>
      as a result of the
      <a href="/wiki/Seventh_Crusade" title="Seventh Crusade">
       Seventh Crusade
      </a>
      . The
      <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">
       Mongol Empire
      </a>
      reached Palestine for the first time in 1260, beginning with the
      <a href="/wiki/Mongol_raids_into_Palestine" title="Mongol raids into Palestine">
       Mongol raids into Palestine
      </a>
      under
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nestorian_Christian" title="Nestorian Christian">
       Nestorian Christian
      </a>
      general
      <a href="/wiki/Kitbuqa" title="Kitbuqa">
       Kitbuqa
      </a>
      and reaching an apex at the pivotal
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ain_Jalut" title="Battle of Ain Jalut">
       Battle of Ain Jalut
      </a>
      . In 1486, hostilities broke out between the Mamluks and the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottoman_Turks" title="Ottoman Turks">
       Ottoman Turks
      </a>
      and the Ottomans captured Palestine in 1516.
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1832, the region was conquered by
      <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali_of_Egypt" title="Muhammad Ali of Egypt">
       Muhammad Ali
      </a>
      's Egypt, but, in 1840, Britain intervened and returned control of the Levant to the Ottomans in return for further
      <a href="/wiki/Capitulations_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Capitulations of the Ottoman Empire">
       capitulations
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2013)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      The turbulent period of Egyptian rule experienced two major revolts (the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1834_Arab_Peasants_revolt" title="1834 Arab Peasants revolt">
       1834 Arab Peasants revolt
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/1838_Druze_revolt" title="1838 Druze revolt">
       1838 Druze revolt
      </a>
      ) and a significant demographic change in coastal areas, populated by Egyptian Arab peasants and former soldiers of Ali. The end of the 19th century saw the beginning of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zionist" title="Zionist">
       Zionist
      </a>
      immigration and the
      <a href="/wiki/Revival_of_the_Hebrew_language" title="Revival of the Hebrew language">
       revival of the Hebrew language
      </a>
      . Increasing Jewish immigration in the late 19th and early 20th centuries added considerably to the Jewish communities in Jerusalem, Safed, Tiberias and Jaffa.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      During
      <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">
       World War I
      </a>
      the
      <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">
       British
      </a>
      government issued the pro-Zionist
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Balfour_Declaration_of_1917" title="Balfour Declaration of 1917">
       Balfour Declaration of 1917
      </a>
      . The British captured Jerusalem a month later. The League of Nations formally awarded Britain a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Mandate_for_Palestine" title="British Mandate for Palestine">
       mandate
      </a>
      over Palestine in 1922. The land west of the Jordan River was under direct British administration until 1948, while the land east of the Jordan was a semi-autonomous region known as Transjordan, under the rule of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hashemite" title="Hashemite">
       Hashemite
      </a>
      family from the Hijaz, and gained independence in 1946. The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1936%E2%80%931939_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine" title="19361939 Arab revolt in Palestine">
       19361939 Arab revolt in Palestine
      </a>
      was a nationalist uprising by Palestinian Arabs against British colonial rule and mass Jewish immigration.
     </p>
     <p>
      After the Nazi
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holocaust" title="Holocaust">
       Holocaust
      </a>
      , pressure grew for the international recognition of a Jewish state in Palestine. In 1947, the British Government announced its intention to terminate the Mandate. The
      <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">
       United Nations
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UN_General_Assembly" title="UN General Assembly">
       General Assembly
      </a>
      voted to partition Palestine into independent Arab and Jewish states, with a special international regime for Jerusalem. The Arabs rejected the partition of Palestine, but the Jews declared the independence of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_of_Israel" title="State of Israel">
       State of Israel
      </a>
      in May 1948. During the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1948_Palestine_War" title="1948 Palestine War">
       1948 Palestine War
      </a>
      , Israel overran far more territory than was proposed by the Partition Plan; Jordan
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Occupation_of_the_West_Bank_and_East_Jerusalem_by_Jordan" title="Occupation of the West Bank and East Jerusalem by Jordan">
       captured the region today known as the West Bank
      </a>
      , while in the
      <a href="/wiki/Gaza_Strip" title="Gaza Strip">
       Gaza Strip
      </a>
      the
      <a href="/wiki/All-Palestine_Government" title="All-Palestine Government">
       All-Palestine Government
      </a>
      was announced in September 1948. In what is known as the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nakba" title="Nakba">
       Nakba
      </a>
      , or "Catastrophe", hundreds of Palestinian villages and over 70,000 Palestinian homes were ruined and destroyed .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/1948_Palestinian_exodus" title="1948 Palestinian exodus">
       700,000 Palestinians
      </a>
      fled or were driven from their native land by the Israelis. The Palestinian refugees were unable to return following the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lausanne_Conference,_1949" title="Lausanne Conference, 1949">
       Lausanne Conference, 1949
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2013)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      During and after the 1948 war, a wave of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jewish_exodus_from_Arab_countries" title="Jewish exodus from Arab countries">
       Jewish refugees from Arab countries
      </a>
      arrived in Palestine, further exacerbating the situation for Palestinian Arabs. The question of the right to return of the refugeees and their descendants remains a source of dispute.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The All-Palestinian Government was later moved from Gaza to Cairo and eventually dissolved in 1959 by Egyptian President Nasser. Gaza was taken into
      <a href="/wiki/Occupation_of_the_Gaza_Strip_by_Egypt" title="Occupation of the Gaza Strip by Egypt">
       Egyptian military administration
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The Palestinian national movement gradually regrouped in the West Bank and Gaza, and in refugee camps in neighbouring Arab states. The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palestine_Liberation_Organisation" title="Palestine Liberation Organisation">
       Palestine Liberation Organisation
      </a>
      (PLO) emerged as its leading umbrella group. During the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Six_Day_War" title="Six Day War">
       Six Day War
      </a>
      in June 1967, Israel seized East Jerusalem and the West Bank from Jordan and Gaza from Egypt, as well as the Golan Heights from Syria. Despite international objections and UN resolutions, Israel began a policy of establishing illegal
      <a href="/wiki/Israeli_settlement" title="Israeli settlement">
       Israeli settlements
      </a>
      in the
      <a href="/wiki/Israeli-occupied_territories" title="Israeli-occupied territories">
       Israeli-occupied territories
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The PLO under
      <a href="/wiki/Yasser_Arafat" title="Yasser Arafat">
       Yasser Arafat
      </a>
      gradually won international recognition as the representative of the Palestinian people. From 1987 to 1993, the
      <a href="/wiki/First_Intifada" title="First Intifada">
       First Palestinian Intifada
      </a>
      against Israel took place, ending with the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1993_Oslo_Peace_Accords" title="1993 Oslo Peace Accords">
       1993 Oslo Peace Accords
      </a>
      . These accords established a
      <a href="/wiki/Palestinian_National_Authority" title="Palestinian National Authority">
       Palestinian National Authority
      </a>
      (PNA - also referred to as the Palestinian Authority, or PA) as an interim body to run parts of Gaza and the West Bank (but not East Jerusalem) pending an agreed solution to the conflict.
     </p>
     <p>
      During the
      <a href="/wiki/Second_Intifada" title="Second Intifada">
       Second Intifada
      </a>
      (2000-2005), Israel withdrew from the Gaza Strip and began building the West Bank
      <a href="/wiki/Israeli_West_Bank_barrier" title="Israeli West Bank barrier">
       barrier
      </a>
      . In 2006,
      <a href="/wiki/Hamas" title="Hamas">
       Hamas
      </a>
      won the Palestinian legislative elections and took control of the Gaza Strip in 2007, triggering the Israeli and Egyptian
      <a href="/wiki/Blockade_of_the_Gaza_Strip" title="Blockade of the Gaza Strip">
       Blockade of the Gaza Strip
      </a>
      (2007-the present). In 2008-09 and again in 2014, Israel bombed millitants in Gaza in response to rocket fire. These operations were however criticized for causing civilian deaths.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In October 2011,
      <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">
       UNESCO
      </a>
      admitted the "
      <a href="/wiki/State_of_Palestine" title="State of Palestine">
       State of Palestine
      </a>
      " as a member in October. In November 2012, the State of Palestine was upgraded in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UN" title="UN">
       UN
      </a>
      to non-member observer state status, a move that allows it to take part in General Assembly debates and improves its chances of joining other UN agencies.
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_period">
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_history" title="Ancient history">
        Ancient period
       </a>
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Palestine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Ancient period">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote">
      Further information:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pre-history_of_the_Southern_Levant" title="Pre-history of the Southern Levant">
       Pre-history of the Southern Levant
      </a>
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Proto-Canaanite_period">
       <a href="/wiki/Canaan" title="Canaan">
        Proto-Canaanite period
       </a>
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Palestine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Proto-Canaanite period">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jerycho8.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1712" data-file-width="2288" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Jerycho8.jpg/220px-Jerycho8.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Jerycho8.jpg/330px-Jerycho8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Jerycho8.jpg/440px-Jerycho8.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jerycho8.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A dwelling unearthed at Tell es-Sultan,
        <a href="/wiki/Jericho" title="Jericho">
         Jericho
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The earliest human remains in Palestine were found in
      <a href="/wiki/Ubeidiya" title="Ubeidiya">
       Ubeidiya
      </a>
      , some 3km south of the
      <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Galilee" title="Sea of Galilee">
       Sea of Galilee
      </a>
      (Lake Tiberias), in the
      <a href="/wiki/Jordan_Rift_Valley" title="Jordan Rift Valley">
       Jordan Rift Valley
      </a>
      . The remains are dated to the
      <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">
       Pleistocene
      </a>
      , c. 1.5 million years ago. These are traces of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_human_migration" title="Early human migration">
       earliest migration
      </a>
      of
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Homo_erectus" title="Homo erectus">
        Homo erectus
       </a>
      </i>
      out of Africa. The site yielded
      <a href="/wiki/Hand_axe" title="Hand axe">
       hand axes
      </a>
      of the
      <a href="/wiki/Acheulean" title="Acheulean">
       Acheulean
      </a>
      type.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Wadi El Amud between
      <a href="/wiki/Safed" title="Safed">
       Safed
      </a>
      and the Sea of Galilee was the site of the first prehistoric dig in Palestine, in 1925. The discovery of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palestine_Man" title="Palestine Man">
       Palestine Man
      </a>
      in the Zuttiyeh Cave in Wadi Al-Amud near
      <a href="/wiki/Safed" title="Safed">
       Safed
      </a>
      in 1925 provided some clues to human development in the area.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qafzeh" title="Qafzeh">
       Qafzeh
      </a>
      is a
      <a href="/wiki/Paleoanthropology" title="Paleoanthropology">
       paleoanthropological
      </a>
      site south of
      <a href="/wiki/Nazareth" title="Nazareth">
       Nazareth
      </a>
      where eleven significant
      <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">
       fossilised
      </a>
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens" title="Homo sapiens">
        Homo sapiens
       </a>
      </i>
      skeletons have been found at the main rock shelter. These
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatomically_modern_humans" title="Anatomically modern humans">
       anatomically modern humans
      </a>
      , both adult and infant, are now dated to about 90100,000 years old, and many of the bones are stained with
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red_ochre" title="Red ochre">
       red ochre
      </a>
      , which is conjectured to have been used in the burial process, a significant indicator of
      <a href="/wiki/Ritual" title="Ritual">
       ritual behavior
      </a>
      and thereby
      <a href="/wiki/Symbol" title="Symbol">
       symbolic
      </a>
      thought and
      <a href="/wiki/Intelligence" title="Intelligence">
       intelligence
      </a>
      . 71 pieces of unused
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red_ochre" title="Red ochre">
       red ochre
      </a>
      also littered the site. Mount Carmel has yielded several important findings, among them
      <a href="/wiki/Kebara_Cave" title="Kebara Cave">
       Kebara Cave
      </a>
      that was inhabited between 60,00048,000
      <a href="/wiki/Before_Present" title="Before Present">
       BP
      </a>
      and where the most complete
      <a href="/wiki/Neanderthal" title="Neanderthal">
       Neanderthal
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Skeleton" title="Skeleton">
       skeleton
      </a>
      found to date. The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tabun,_Israel" title="Tabun, Israel">
       Tabun
      </a>
      cave was occupied intermittently during the Lower and Middle
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paleolithic_age" title="Paleolithic age">
       Paleolithic ages
      </a>
      (500,000 to around 40,000 years ago).
      <a href="/wiki/Excavation_(archaeology)" title="Excavation (archaeology)">
       Excavations
      </a>
      suggest that it features one of the longest sequences of human occupation in the
      <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">
       Levant
      </a>
      . In the nearby
      <a href="/wiki/Es_Skhul" title="Es Skhul">
       Es Skhul
      </a>
      cave excavations revealed the first evidence of the late Epipalaeolithic
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natufian" title="Natufian">
       Natufian
      </a>
      culture, characterized by the presence of abundant
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microliths" title="Microliths">
       microliths
      </a>
      , human burials and ground stone tools. This also represents one area where
      <a href="/wiki/Neanderthal" title="Neanderthal">
       Neanderthals
      </a>
      present in the region from 200,000 to 45,000 years agolived alongside modern humans dating to 100,000 years ago.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the caves of
      <a href="/wiki/Shuqba" title="Shuqba">
       Shuqba
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Ramallah" title="Ramallah">
       Ramallah
      </a>
      and Wadi Khareitun in
      <a href="/wiki/Bethlehem" title="Bethlehem">
       Bethlehem
      </a>
      , stone, wood and animal bone tools were found and attributed to the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natufian" title="Natufian">
       Natufian
      </a>
      culture (c. 12,80010,300 BCE). Other remains from this era have been found at Tel Abu Hureura, Ein Mallaha, Beidha and
      <a href="/wiki/Jericho" title="Jericho">
       Jericho
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Between 10,000 and 5000 BCE, agricultural communities were established. Evidence of such settlements were found at Tel es-Sultan in Jericho and consisted of a number of walls, a religious shrine, and a 23-foot (7.0m) tower with an internal staircase
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
       <a href="#cite_note-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
       <a href="#cite_note-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Jericho is believed to be one of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_oldest_continuously_inhabited_cities" title="List of oldest continuously inhabited cities">
       oldest continuously-inhabited cities in the world
      </a>
      , with evidence of settlement dating back to 9000 BCE, providing important information about early human habitation in the
      <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">
       Near East
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
       <a href="#cite_note-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Along the Jericho
      <a href="/wiki/Dead_Sea" title="Dead Sea">
       Dead Sea
      </a>
      
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beersheva" title="Beersheva">
       Bir es-Saba
      </a>
      
      <a href="/wiki/Gaza_City" title="Gaza City">
       Gaza
      </a>
      
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sinai" title="Sinai">
       Sinai
      </a>
      route, a culture originating in
      <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">
       Syria
      </a>
      , marked by the use of copper and stone tools, brought new migrant groups to the region contributing to an increasingly urban fabric.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shahinp4_19-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Shahinp4-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
       <a href="#cite_note-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
       <a href="#cite_note-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      By the early Bronze Age (30002200 BCE), independent
      <a href="/wiki/Canaan" title="Canaan">
       Canaanite
      </a>
      city-states situated in plains and coastal regions and surrounded by mud-brick defensive walls were established and most of these cities relied on nearby agricultural hamlets for their food needs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shahinp4_19-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Shahinp4-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
       <a href="#cite_note-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Archaeological finds from the early Canaanite era have been found at
      <a href="/wiki/Tel_Megiddo" title="Tel Megiddo">
       Tel Megiddo
      </a>
      , Jericho, Tel al-Far'a (Gaza),
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bet_Shean" title="Bet Shean">
       Bisan
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Ai_(Bible)" title="Ai (Bible)">
       Ai
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Deir_Dibwan" title="Deir Dibwan">
       Deir Dibwan
      </a>
      /
      <a href="/wiki/Ramallah_and_al-Bireh_Governorate" title="Ramallah and al-Bireh Governorate">
       Ramallah District
      </a>
      ), Tel an Nasbe (
      <a href="/wiki/Al-Bireh" title="Al-Bireh">
       al-Bireh
      </a>
      ) and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jib_(village)" title="Jib (village)">
       Jib
      </a>
      (Jerusalem). The Canaanite city-states held trade and diplomatic relations with
      <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">
       Egypt
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">
       Syria
      </a>
      . Parts of the Canaanite urban civilization were destroyed around 2300 BCE, though there is no consensus as to why. Incursions by nomads from the east of the
      <a href="/wiki/Jordan_River" title="Jordan River">
       Jordan River
      </a>
      who settled in the hills followed soon thereafter.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shahinp4_19-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Shahinp4-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
       <a href="#cite_note-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Middle_Bronze_Age" title="Middle Bronze Age">
       Middle Bronze Age
      </a>
      (22001500 BCE),
      <a href="/wiki/Canaan" title="Canaan">
       Canaan
      </a>
      was influenced by the surrounding civilizations of ancient Egypt,
      <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">
       Mesopotamia
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">
       Phoenicia
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Minoan_civilization" title="Minoan civilization">
       Minoan
      </a>
      Crete, and Syria. Diverse commercial ties and an agriculturally based economy led to the development of new pottery forms, the cultivation of grapes, and the extensive use of bronze.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shahinp4_19-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Shahinp4-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MiddleBronzeAge_24-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-MiddleBronzeAge-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Burial customs from this time seemed to be influenced by a belief in the afterlife.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shahinp4_19-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Shahinp4-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Middle_Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="Middle Kingdom of Egypt">
       Middle Kingdom Egyptian
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Execration_Texts" title="Execration Texts">
       Execration Texts
      </a>
      attest to Canaanite trade with Egypt during this period.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Slavik2001_p.60_26-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Slavik2001_p.60-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MazarB_p.45_27-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-MazarB_p.45-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Minoan influence is apparent at
      <a href="/wiki/Tel_Kabri" title="Tel Kabri">
       Tel Kabri
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
       <a href="#cite_note-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="New_Kingdom_Egyptian_period">
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Kingdom" title="New Kingdom">
        New Kingdom Egyptian
       </a>
       period
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Palestine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: New Kingdom Egyptian period">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Egypt_1450_BC.svg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="1300" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Egypt_1450_BC.svg/220px-Egypt_1450_BC.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Egypt_1450_BC.svg/330px-Egypt_1450_BC.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Egypt_1450_BC.svg/440px-Egypt_1450_BC.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Egypt_1450_BC.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        New Kingdom at its maximum territorial extent in the 15th century BCE
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:14_century_BC_Eastern.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="578" data-file-width="1041" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/14_century_BC_Eastern.png/350px-14_century_BC_Eastern.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/14_century_BC_Eastern.png/525px-14_century_BC_Eastern.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/14_century_BC_Eastern.png/700px-14_century_BC_Eastern.png 2x" width="350"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:14_century_BC_Eastern.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Map of the
        <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Near_East" title="Ancient Near East">
         Ancient Near East
        </a>
        during the
        <a href="/wiki/Amarna_Period" title="Amarna Period">
         Amarna Period
        </a>
        , showing the great powers of the day: Egypt (orange),
        <a href="/wiki/Hittites" title="Hittites">
         Hatti
        </a>
        (blue), the
        <a href="/wiki/Kassites" title="Kassites">
         Kassite
        </a>
        kingdom of Babylon (black),
        <a href="/wiki/Middle_Assyrian_Empire" title="Middle Assyrian Empire">
         Middle Assyrian Empire
        </a>
        (yellow), and Mitanni (brown). The extent of the Achaean/Mycenaean civilization is shown in purple.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      During 15501400 BCE, the Canaanite cities became vassals to Egypt as the Egyptian
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Kingdom" title="New Kingdom">
       New Kingdom
      </a>
      reunited Egypt and expanded into the
      <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">
       Levant
      </a>
      under
      <a href="/wiki/Ahmose_I" title="Ahmose I">
       Ahmose I
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Thutmose_I" title="Thutmose I">
       Thutmose I
      </a>
      . Political, commercial and military events towards the end of this period (14501350 BCE) were recorded by ambassadors and Canaanite proxy rulers for Egypt in 379 cuneiform tablets known as the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amarna_Letters" title="Amarna Letters">
       Amarna Letters
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
       <a href="#cite_note-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      These refer to several local proxy rulers for Egypt such as
      <a href="/wiki/Biridiya" title="Biridiya">
       Biridiya
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Tel_Megiddo" title="Tel Megiddo">
       Megiddo
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lib%27ayu" title="Lib'ayu">
       Lib'ayu
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Shechem" title="Shechem">
       Shechem
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Abdi-Heba" title="Abdi-Heba">
       Abdi-Heba
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">
       Jerusalem
      </a>
      . In the first year of his reign pharaoh
      <a href="/wiki/Seti_I" title="Seti I">
       Seti I
      </a>
      (ca.1294-1290 BCE) waged a campaign to resubordinate Canaan to Egyptian rule, thrusting north as far as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beit_Shean" title="Beit Shean">
       Beit Shean
      </a>
      , and installing local vassals to administer the area in his name. A burial site yielding a
      <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Scarab" title="Scarab">
       scarab
      </a>
      bearing his name, found within a Canaanite coffin excavated in the
      <a href="/wiki/Jezreel_Valley" title="Jezreel Valley">
       Jezreel Valley
      </a>
      , attests to Egypt's presence in the area.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
       <a href="#cite_note-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Excavations have established that the late 13th, the 12th and the early 11th centuries BCE witnessed the foundation of perhaps hundreds of insignificant, unprotected village settlements, many in the mountains of Palestine.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LemcheJHS_31-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-LemcheJHS-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      From around the 11th century BCE, there was a reduction in the number of villages, though this was counterbalanced by the rise of certain settlements to the status of fortified townships.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LemcheJHS_31-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-LemcheJHS-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1178 BCE, the
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Djahy" title="Battle of Djahy">
       Battle of Djahy (Canaan)
      </a>
      between
      <a href="/wiki/Ramesses_III" title="Ramesses III">
       Ramesses III
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Sea_Peoples" title="Sea Peoples">
       Sea Peoples
      </a>
      marked the beginning of the decline in power of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Kingdom" title="New Kingdom">
       New Kingdom
      </a>
      in the
      <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">
       Levant
      </a>
      during the wider
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bronze_Age_collapse" title="Bronze Age collapse">
       Bronze Age collapse
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2011)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div style="clear:both;">
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Independent_Israelite.2C_Philistine_and_Canaanite_period">
       Independent
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Israelite" title="Israelite">
        Israelite
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philistine" title="Philistine">
        Philistine
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Canaan" title="Canaan">
        Canaanite
       </a>
       period
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Palestine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Independent Israelite, Philistine and Canaanite period">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      During the beginning of the
      <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">
       Iron Age
      </a>
      (c. 1175 BCE), the
      <a href="/wiki/Philistines" title="Philistines">
       Philistines
      </a>
      occupied the southern coast of Canaan, and mingled with the local population, losing their separate identity over several generations.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shahinp6_32-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Shahinp6-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
       <a href="#cite_note-33">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        33
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Pottery remains found in
      <a href="/wiki/As-e_Qadim" title="As-e Qadim">
       As
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Gath_(city)" title="Gath (city)">
       Gath (city)
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Ekron" title="Ekron">
       Ekron
      </a>
      and Gaza decorated with stylized birds provided the first archaeological evidence for Philistine settlement in the region. The Philistines are credited with introducing iron weapons and chariots to the local population.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
       <a href="#cite_note-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Modern archaeologists dispute parts of the Biblical tradition.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
       <a href="#cite_note-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In The Bible Unearthed Finkelstein and Silberman describe how, up until 1967, the Israelite heartland in the highlands of western Palestine was virtually an archaeological 'terra incognita'. Since then the traditional territories of the tribes of Judah, Benjamin, Ephraim, and Manasseh have been covered by intensive surveys. These surveys have revealed the sudden emergence of a new culture contrasting with the Philistine and Canaanite societies existing in Palestine during Iron Age I.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Finkelstein_3-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Finkelstein-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This new culture is characterised by the lack of pork remains (whereas pork formed 20% of the Philistine diet in places), an abandonment of the Philistines/Canaanite custom of having highly decorated pottery, and the practice of circumcision. According to Prof. Faust Avraham of
      <a href="/wiki/Bar-Ilan_University" title="Bar-Ilan University">
       Bar-Ilan University
      </a>
      , the Israelite ethnic identity had been created, not from the Exodus and a subsequent conquest, but from a transformation of the existing Canaanite-Philistine cultures.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
       <a href="#cite_note-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <blockquote>
      <p>
       Finkelstein and Silberman write: "These surveys revolutionized the study of early Israel. The discovery of the remains of a dense network of highland villages  all apparently established within the span of few generations  indicated that a dramatic social transformation had taken place in the central hill country of Canaan around 1200 BCE. There was no sign of violent invasion or even the infiltration of a clearly defined ethnic group. Instead, it seemed to be a revolution in lifestyle. In the formerly sparsely populated highlands from the Judean hills in the south to the hills of Samaria in the north, far from the Canaanite cities that were in the process of collapse and disintegration, about two-hundred fifty hilltop communities suddenly sprang up. Here were the first Israelites."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         37
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      From then on, over a period of hundreds of years until after the return of the exiles from Babylon, the Canaanites were gradually absorbed by the Israelites until after the period of Ezra (~450 BCE) when there is no more biblical record of them.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
       <a href="#cite_note-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Hebrew, a dialect of Canaanite became the language of the hill country and later the valleys and plains.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
       <a href="#cite_note-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The first use of
      <a href="/wiki/Grapheme" title="Grapheme">
       grapheme
      </a>
      -based
      <a href="/wiki/Proto-Sinaitic_script" title="Proto-Sinaitic script">
       writing originated in the area
      </a>
      , probably among Canaanite peoples resident in Egypt. All modern alphabets are descended from this writing. Written evidence of the use of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classical_Hebrew" title="Classical Hebrew">
       Classical Hebrew
      </a>
      exists from about 1000 BCE. It was written using the
      <a href="/wiki/Paleo-Hebrew_alphabet" title="Paleo-Hebrew alphabet">
       Paleo-Hebrew alphabet
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      According to
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Hebrew_Bible" title="The Hebrew Bible">
       the Hebrew Bible
      </a>
      , the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_Monarchy" title="United Monarchy">
       United Kingdom of Israel
      </a>
      was established by the Israelite tribes with
      <a href="/wiki/Saul" title="Saul">
       Saul
      </a>
      as its first king in 1020 BCE.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MFA_40-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-MFA-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1000 BCE, Jerusalem was made the capital of King
      <a href="/wiki/David" title="David">
       David
      </a>
      's kingdom and it is believed that the
      <a href="/wiki/Temple_in_Jerusalem" title="Temple in Jerusalem">
       First Temple
      </a>
      was constructed in this period by
      <a href="/wiki/Solomon" title="Solomon">
       King Solomon
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MFA_40-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-MFA-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      By 930 BCE, the united kingdom split to form the northern
      <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Israel_(Samaria)" title="Kingdom of Israel (Samaria)">
       Kingdom of Israel
      </a>
      , and the southern
      <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Judah" title="Kingdom of Judah">
       Kingdom of Judah
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MFA_40-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-MFA-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      These kingdoms coexisted with several more kingdoms in the greater Palestine area, including
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philistine" title="Philistine">
       Philistine
      </a>
      town states on the Southwestern Mediterranean coast,
      <a href="/wiki/Edom" title="Edom">
       Edom
      </a>
      , to the South of
      <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Judah" title="Kingdom of Judah">
       Judah
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Moab" title="Moab">
       Moab
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Ammon" title="Ammon">
       Ammon
      </a>
      to the East of the
      <a href="/wiki/Jordan_River" title="Jordan River">
       river Jordan.
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
       <a href="#cite_note-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The socio-political system during this period was characterized by local patrons fighting other local patrons, lasting until around the mid-9th century BCE when some local chieftains were able to create large political structures that exceeded the boundaries of those present in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bronze_Age_Levant#Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age Levant">
       Late Bronze Age Levant
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LemcheJHS_31-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-LemcheJHS-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Archaeological evidence from this era is believed to corroborate some Biblical events. In 925 BCE, Pharaoh
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sheshonk_I" title="Sheshonk I">
       Sheshonk I
      </a>
      of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Third_Intermediate_Period" title="Third Intermediate Period">
       Third Intermediate Period
      </a>
      is recorded to have invaded
      <a href="/wiki/Canaan" title="Canaan">
       Canaan
      </a>
      following the
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bitter_Lakes" title="Battle of Bitter Lakes">
       Battle of Bitter Lakes
      </a>
      , and is thought to be the same as
      <a href="/wiki/Shishak" title="Shishak">
       Shishak
      </a>
      , the first Pharaoh mentioned in the
      <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">
       Bible
      </a>
      who captured and pillaged Jerusalem. There was an at least partial Egyptian withdrawal from Palestine in this period, though it is likely that
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bet_Shean" title="Bet Shean">
       Bet Shean
      </a>
      was an Egyptian garrison as late as the beginning of the 10th century BCE.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LemcheJHS_31-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-LemcheJHS-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kurkh_Monolith" title="Kurkh Monolith">
       Kurkh Monolith
      </a>
      , dated c. 835 BCE, describes King
      <a href="/wiki/Shalmaneser_III" title="Shalmaneser III">
       Shalmaneser III
      </a>
      of Assyria's
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Qarqar" title="Battle of Qarqar">
       Battle of Qarqar
      </a>
      , where he fought alongside the contingents of several kings, among them
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_Ahab" title="King Ahab">
       King Ahab
      </a>
      and King
      <a href="/wiki/Gindibu" title="Gindibu">
       Gindibu
      </a>
      . The Mesha Stele, from c. 850 BCE, recounts the conquering of
      <a href="/wiki/Moab" title="Moab">
       Moab
      </a>
      , located East of the
      <a href="/wiki/Dead_Sea" title="Dead Sea">
       Dead Sea
      </a>
      , by king
      <a href="/wiki/Omri" title="Omri">
       Omri
      </a>
      , and the successful revolt of Moabian king
      <a href="/wiki/Mesha" title="Mesha">
       Mesha
      </a>
      against Omri's son, presumably
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_Ahab" title="King Ahab">
       King Ahab
      </a>
      (and French scholar
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Andr%C3%A9_Lemaire&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Andr Lemaire (page does not exist)">
       Andr Lemaire
      </a>
      reported that line 31 of the Stele bears the phrase "the house of David" (in
      <i>
       Biblical Archaeology Review
      </i>
      [May/June 1994], pp.3037).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jewishhistory.com_42-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-jewishhistory.com-42">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      ). Inscriptions at
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tel_Dan" title="Tel Dan">
       Tel Dan
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Tell_es-Safi" title="Tell es-Safi">
       Tell es-Safi
      </a>
      record parts of the conquest of the region by
      <a href="/wiki/Hazael" title="Hazael">
       Hazael
      </a>
      of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aram_Damascus" title="Aram Damascus">
       Aram Damascus
      </a>
      in the 830s BCE.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2011)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kingdoms_of_the_Levant_Map_830.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2332" data-file-width="1955" height="262" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Kingdoms_of_the_Levant_Map_830.png/220px-Kingdoms_of_the_Levant_Map_830.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Kingdoms_of_the_Levant_Map_830.png/330px-Kingdoms_of_the_Levant_Map_830.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Kingdoms_of_the_Levant_Map_830.png/440px-Kingdoms_of_the_Levant_Map_830.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kingdoms_of_the_Levant_Map_830.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The Levant c. 830 BCE
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Developments in Palestine during this period have been the focus of debate between those who accept the version in the Hebrew Bible of the conquest of Canaan by the Israelite tribes, and those who reject it.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ladislau_43-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ladislau-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Niels Peter Lemche, of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Copenhagen_School_(theology)" title="The Copenhagen School (theology)">
       Copenhagen School
      </a>
      of Biblical Studies, submits that the biblical picture of ancient Israel "is contrary to any image of ancient Palestinian society that can be established on the basis of ancient sources from Palestine or referring to Palestine and that there is no way this image in the Bible can be reconciled with the historical past of the region".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LemcheJHS_31-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-LemcheJHS-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      For example, according to Jon Schiller and Hermann Austel, among others, while in the past, the Bible story was seen as historical truth, "a growing number of archaeological scholars, particularly those of the
      <a href="/wiki/Biblical_Minimalism" title="Biblical Minimalism">
       minimalist
      </a>
      school, are now insisting that Kings David and Solomon are 'no more real than
      <a href="/wiki/King_Arthur" title="King Arthur">
       King Arthur
      </a>
      ,' citing the lack of archaeological evidence attesting to the existence of the United Kingdom of Israel, and the unreliability of biblical texts, due to their being composed in a much later period."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grisantip160_44-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Grisantip160-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schillerp98_45-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Schillerp98-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Sites and
      <a href="/wiki/Artifact_(archaeology)" title="Artifact (archaeology)">
       artifacts
      </a>
      , including the
      <a href="/wiki/Large_Stone_Structure" title="Large Stone Structure">
       Large Stone Structure
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Mount_Ebal" title="Mount Ebal">
       Mount Ebal
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Merneptah_Stele" title="Merneptah Stele">
       Merneptah
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Mesha_Stele" title="Mesha Stele">
       Mesha
      </a>
      stelae, among others, are subject to widely varying historical interpretations: the "conservative camp" reconstructs the history of Israel according to the biblical text and views archaeological evidence in that context, while scholars in the minimalist or deconstructionist school hold that there is no archaeological evidence supporting the idea of a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_Monarchy" title="United Monarchy">
       United Monarchy
      </a>
      (or
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Israelite" title="Israelite">
       Israelite
      </a>
      nation) and the biblical account is a religious mythology created by Judean scribes in the Persian and Hellenistic periods; a third camp of centrist scholars acknowledges the value of some isolated elements of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pentateuch" title="Pentateuch">
       Pentateuch
      </a>
      and of Deuteronomonistic accounts as potentially valid history of monarchic times that can be in accord with the archaeological evidence, but argue that nevertheless the biblical narrative should be understood as highly ideological and adapted to the needs of the community at the time of its compilation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
       <a href="#cite_note-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimesErlanger_47-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-nytimesErlanger-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
       <a href="#cite_note-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
       <a href="#cite_note-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
       <a href="#cite_note-50">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        50
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
       <a href="#cite_note-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Neo-Assyrian_and_Neo-Babylonian_Empires_period">
       <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">
        Neo-Assyrian
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Neo-Babylonian_Empire" title="Neo-Babylonian Empire">
        Neo-Babylonian Empires
       </a>
       period
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Palestine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Neo-Assyrian and Neo-Babylonian Empires period">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Assyria.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1363" data-file-width="1983" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Map_of_Assyria.png/220px-Map_of_Assyria.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Map_of_Assyria.png/330px-Map_of_Assyria.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Map_of_Assyria.png/440px-Map_of_Assyria.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Assyria.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Neo-Assyrian Empire at its greatest extent
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Assyrian inscriptions from c. 740 BCE record the military victories of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tiglath_Pileser_III" title="Tiglath Pileser III">
       Tiglath Pileser III
      </a>
      in the region, during which period the
      <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">
       Neo-Assyrian Empire
      </a>
      conquered most of the
      <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">
       Levant
      </a>
      . The Bible records the Israelite cities becoming
      <a href="/wiki/Vassal" title="Vassal">
       vassals
      </a>
      to the
      <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">
       Neo-Assyrian Empire
      </a>
      during this period. At around this time, the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siege_of_Gezer_(c._733_BCE)" title="Siege of Gezer (c. 733 BCE)">
       Siege of Gezer (c. 733 BCE)
      </a>
      , 20 miles (32km) west of Jerusalem, is recorded on a stone relief at the Assyrian royal palace in Nimrud. Further military expeditions into the region are recorded in the annals of
      <a href="/wiki/Sargon_II" title="Sargon II">
       Sargon
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Sennacherib" title="Sennacherib">
       Sennacherib
      </a>
      , as well as in the bible. According to the bible, between 722 and 720 BCE the northern Kingdom of Israel was destroyed by the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrian_Empire" title="Assyrian Empire">
       Assyrian Empire
      </a>
      and the Israelite tribesthereafter known as the
      <a href="/wiki/Ten_Lost_Tribes" title="Ten Lost Tribes">
       Lost Tribes
      </a>
      were exiled.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MFA_40-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-MFA-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The most important finding from the southern Kingdom of Judah is the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siloam_Inscription" title="Siloam Inscription">
       Siloam Inscription
      </a>
      , dated c. 700 BCE, which celebrates the successful encounter of diggers, digging from both sides of the Jerusalem wall to create the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hezekiah%27s_Tunnel" title="Hezekiah's Tunnel">
       Hezekiah water tunnel
      </a>
      and water pool, mentioned in the Hebrew Bible, in
      <a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/bibleversefinder/?book=2Kings&amp;verse=20:20&amp;src=KJV">
       2Kings 20:20
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
       <a href="#cite_note-52">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        52
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
       <a href="#cite_note-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
       <a href="#cite_note-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
       <a href="#cite_note-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">
       Neo-Assyrian Empire
      </a>
      was replaced with the
      <a href="/wiki/Neo-Babylonian_Empire" title="Neo-Babylonian Empire">
       Neo-Babylonian Empire
      </a>
      in c. 627 BCE, following the death of
      <a href="/wiki/Ashurbanipal" title="Ashurbanipal">
       Ashurbanipal
      </a>
      and the successful revolt of
      <a href="/wiki/Nabopolassar" title="Nabopolassar">
       Nabopolassar
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2011)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The region was controlled briefly by Pharaoh
      <a href="/wiki/Necho_II" title="Necho II">
       Necho II
      </a>
      of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twenty-sixth_dynasty_of_Egypt" title="Twenty-sixth dynasty of Egypt">
       Twenty-sixth dynasty of Egypt
      </a>
      between the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Megiddo_(609_BCE)" title="Battle of Megiddo (609 BCE)">
       Battle of Megiddo (609 BCE)
      </a>
      and the
